Later equal-opening conclusions may use these side roles because each role has been earned by the construction.

Theorem 079 — Radii and Neighbouring Chord Share Preserved Unit Length

Claim: The two radii and the neighbouring chord share the preserved unit length.

The side roles inside the central triangle now share their unit evidence. Each radius is a center-to-boundary reach by the fixed opening. The neighbouring chord is the boundary-to-boundary step made by that same opening. The proof compares the witnesses, not the appearance of the sides. Since all three sides are carried by the preserved unit opening, they share the same source length evidence. What crystallises is unit-length preservation across the two radii and the neighbouring chord. This licenses the equal-opening status of the central triangle while still refusing imported angle or equilateral-triangle theory beyond the evidence supplied.

Theorem 080 — Central Triangle Is an Equilateral Cell of the First Structure

Claim: Each central triangle is an equilateral cell of the first structure.

The central triangle can now be named as an equilateral cell of the first structure. The proof has already shown its three station-pairs and has identified the same fixed-opening witness on all three sides. That is enough for equal-opening cell status. The theorem does not reach for the full school package of equilateral triangles; it keeps the claim at the level the construction has paid for. What stands is the first structure’s central triangle as an equilateral cell by source evidence. Later cell assembly may count and combine these cells, but only through the preserved unit relations that define them.

Theorem 081 — Six Neighbouring Boundary Pairs Produce Six Central Equilateral Cells

Claim: The six neighbouring boundary pairs produce six central equilateral cells.

The six neighbouring boundary pairs now produce six central equilateral cells. Boundary closure supplies six adjacent pairs around the cycle, and each neighbouring pair with O forms a central triangle. Each such triangle has the same fixed-opening evidence on its three sides, so each is an equilateral cell in the source sense. The proof moves around the boundary one pair at a time until the cycle returns to its start. What becomes available is the full set of six central equilateral cells. This is cell production by boundary order and unit evidence, not by imported regular-hexagon theory.

Theorem 082 — Six Central Cells Gather Around O

Claim: The six central cells gather around O.

The six central cells gather around O. The proof now reads the produced cells as a surrounding arrangement, not as isolated pieces. Each cell uses O and one neighbouring boundary pair; as the pairs move around the closed boundary, the cells meet around the same center station. The gathering is therefore forced by shared center-use and boundary order. No general symmetry claim is needed. What stands is an around-O assembly of the six central cells. Later paragraphs may read this assembly as a body, but the present permission is simply the lawful gathering of the six source-earned cells around their common center.

Theorem 083 — Six Central Cells Share O as Common Centre Vertex

Claim: The six central cells share O as common centre vertex.

The six central cells share O as their common centre vertex. The proof keeps the shared point explicit. Every cell was formed by taking O together with a neighbouring boundary pair, so O belongs to each cell in the same central role. This does not make a general theorem about all figures with common vertices. It records the source relation of this particular six-cell arrangement. What becomes available is common-centre vertex language: the six cells can be compared and assembled because they all meet at O. That shared vertex will carry later body and decomposition readings without requiring imported symmetry.

Theorem 084 — Adjacent Central Cells Share One Radius

Claim: Adjacent central cells share one radius.

Adjacent central cells share one radius. Consecutive cells around O are built from neighbouring boundary pairs that overlap in one boundary station. Both cells include O, and both include the side from O to that shared boundary station. The proof therefore identifies one center-to-boundary reach as common to the adjacent cells. This is a precise shared-side relation, not a general adjacency doctrine. What becomes available is radius-sharing between neighbouring central cells. Later assembly can use those shared radii as seams, while the proof still refuses any property not carried by the actual station-pair relation.

Theorem 085 — Six Central Cells Form the Central Hexagonal Body Around O

Claim: The six central cells form the central hexagonal body around O.

The six central cells now form the central hexagonal body around O. Each cell is an equal-opening triangle, the six cells gather around the common center vertex, and adjacent cells share radii as seams. The boundary cycle supplies the outside order, while O anchors the inside. The proof therefore integrates the six local cells into one central body without claiming more than the construction gives. It does not import regular-hexagon theory, area formulae, or angle measure. What crystallises is the central hexagonal body as an assembled source object, available for later decomposition, chordal reading, and controlled field arguments.

Theorem 086 — Regular Central Hexagon Read as Six Equilateral Cells

Claim: The regular central hexagon can be read as six equilateral cells.

The eighty-sixth theorem rereads the central hexagon through the six cells that have already been earned. The boundary cycle, the central turn, the adjacent boundary pairs, and the equal-opening cell evidence are all active before the hexagon is named. The proof may therefore see the hexagonal body as six equilateral cells meeting around the center, but it may not use a school hexagon to justify those cells. The figure is regular only in the source-bound sense supplied by repeated equal-opening relations. What becomes available is a six-cell hexagon reading. What remains refused is any imported hexagon theory not carried by the constructed cells.

Theorem 087 — Central Hexagon Has Six Unit Boundary Chords

Claim: The central hexagon has six unit boundary chords.

The eighty-seventh theorem gives the central hexagon its boundary evidence. The six boundary stations are ordered by the six-step cycle, and each adjacent pair is connected by one fixed-opening boundary step. Those six adjacent station-pairs are the six boundary chords of the central hexagon. Each chord is unit because each is witnessed by the preserved opening, not because a regular hexagon theorem has been imported. The proof therefore lets the hexagon carry six unit boundary chords as a constructional fact. What now stands is a bounded six-chord record. General chord theory, angle measure, and perimeter formulae remain outside the claim.

Theorem 088 — Central Hexagon Has Six Unit Radii From O

Claim: The central hexagon has six unit radii from O.

The eighty-eighth theorem pairs the same hexagonal body with its center. Each boundary station was reached from O by the fixed opening, and the six selected boundary stations remain available in the closed cycle. The connector from O to any one of them is therefore a unit radius by source evidence. Six such center-to-boundary reaches stand in the central hexagon. The proof does not need analytic radius language or a circle theorem to make the relation lawful; it spends the original sweep and the selected station record. What becomes available is a six-radius reading from O. Nothing beyond those earned center-to-boundary relations is granted.

Theorem 089 — Central Hexagon as Bounded Lawful Cell Assembly

Claim: The central hexagon is a bounded assembly of lawful cells.

The eighty-ninth theorem sets a boundary around assembly itself. The central hexagon is made of lawful cells, but that does not mean any visible cluster may count as a body. Its boundary chords are earned by adjacent station steps, its radii are earned by the center-to-boundary reach, and its six central cells are earned by the turn field. Those pieces now gather into a bounded assembly whose parts can be checked against the source record. What crystallises is a lawful central body rather than a decorative outline. Later proof may decompose or count it, but it may not treat unsupported visual resemblance as assembly evidence.

Theorem 090 — Central Cell as Native Area Seed

Claim: The central cell is the native area seed.

The ninetieth theorem lets area begin at the smallest lawful scale. The central cell already has three sides witnessed by one fixed opening and has become the substrate’s first native equal-opening triangle. It can now serve as the seed from which later area readings grow. This does not yet produce a general area formula, nor does it import Euclidean area theory. It only says that the first cell is a stable unit of surface-reading because its boundary and side evidence have been earned. What becomes available is native area seedhood: later decompositions may count or compare cells by returning to this first proof-bearing unit.

Theorem 091 — Central Cell as Proof-Carrying Unit

Claim: The central cell is also a proof-carrying unit.

The ninety-first theorem refuses to let the central cell become merely a patch of surface. The same cell that can seed area also carries proof: its three station-pairs, equal-opening side witnesses, and place in the turn field remain active. A later argument may therefore use the cell both as a unit of decomposition and as a unit of relation. The proof does not add a new geometric theory; it recognizes the double role already earned. What stands is a proof-carrying unit, a cell whose shape, sides, and source witnesses travel together. What remains refused is treating area pieces as mute regions with their proof history erased.

Theorem 092 — Equilateral-Cell Recurrence Allows First Triangle Proof to Travel

Claim: Equilateral-cell recurrence allows the first triangle proof to travel.

The ninety-second theorem explains how the first triangle proof can travel. Equilateral cells recur only where the construction preserves the relevant station roles and equal-opening side evidence. When those conditions recur, the proof of the first native equal-opening triangle may be reused in the new cell. The theorem does not import congruence, similarity, or symmetry as mature doctrines. It spends transportability under preserved defining relations. What becomes available is travelling triangle proof: the field may recognize recurring equal-opening cells without proving the same side witnesses from nothing each time. What remains refused is copying the first triangle into places where its source conditions have not survived.

Theorem 093 — Central Cell Read by Sides, Roles, Adjacency, Orientation, and Area

Claim: A central cell can be read by sides, roles, adjacency, orientation, and area.

The ninety-third theorem gives the central cell several lawful readings without letting them collapse into one another. Its sides are station-pairs witnessed by the fixed opening. Its roles come from center and boundary stations. Its adjacency appears when neighbouring cells share a center-bound side. Its orientation comes from its place in the ordered turn. Its area role begins from its status as native seed. The proof gathers these readings around one cell while keeping each reading source-bound. What becomes available is a richer cell vocabulary. What remains refused is using one reading to smuggle in another before the relevant relation has been earned.

Theorem 094 — Central Six-Cell Assembly as First Complete Local Body

Claim: The central six-cell assembly is the first complete local body.

The ninety-fourth theorem names the first complete local body. Six central cells have gathered around O, their shared sides have been identified, and the boundary cycle has closed without requiring a seventh station. The resulting assembly is local because it belongs to the first around-center field; it is complete because the six-cell turn leaves no central gap. This is not the whole triangular field and not all plane geometry. It is the first bounded body whose internal cell structure is fully source-readable. What becomes available is a complete local body for later decomposition, catalogue, and comparison. Wider field claims remain for later proof.

Theorem 095 — Central Body Decomposed Along Six Radii

Claim: The central body can be decomposed along its six radii.

The ninety-fifth theorem lets the central body be cut by its own evidence. The six radii from O to the boundary stations are already earned, and each adjacent pair of radii bounds one central cell with the boundary chord between their endpoints. Decomposing the body along those radii therefore does not add arbitrary cuts. It follows the source structure that made the body in the first place. What becomes available is radial decomposition of the central body into six equal-opening cells. What remains refused is any decomposition that ignores the center, station order, or side witnesses and treats the hexagon as an unearned visual region.

Theorem 096 — Central Body Read by Boundary, Cell, Chord, Diameter, and Symmetry

Claim: The central body can be read by boundary, cell, chord, diameter, and symmetry.

The ninety-sixth theorem gathers several readings of the central body into one disciplined inventory. Its boundary comes from the six unit chords. Its cells come from the six central equal-opening triangles. Its longer connectors include chords and through-center diameter relations where opposite stations have been earned. Its symmetry may be noticed only as preserved relation-pattern, not as a ready-made group theory. The proof lets boundary, cell, chord, diameter, and symmetry language touch the body, but each term remains tied to its source witness. What becomes available is a multi-reading central body. What remains refused is mature geometric vocabulary detached from the construction.

Theorem 097 — Six Boundary Stations Make Boundary-Triangle Selection Possible

Claim: Six boundary stations make boundary-triangle selection possible.

The ninety-seventh theorem turns the six boundary stations into a selection field. Until the stations exist, a boundary triangle cannot be chosen. Once six lawful stations stand on the first circle, the proof may select triples from them and ask what triangle instances those triples produce. The theorem does not yet classify the triangles or count all identities. It simply opens the selection possibility. What becomes available is boundary-triangle selection from six earned stations. What remains refused is treating an arbitrary drawn triangle as present before its three stations have been selected from the source field.

Theorem 098 — Three Boundary Stations Produce a Boundary Triangle Instance

Claim: Choosing three boundary stations produces a boundary triangle instance.

The ninety-eighth theorem performs one boundary-triangle selection. Three boundary stations are chosen from the sixfold station record, and the joins among them form one boundary triangle instance. The proof does not yet decide its family, measure its angles, or compare it with other instances. It only says that a selected triple can produce a triangle instance once the stations and joins are available. That modest result matters because catalogue work must begin with instances, not with abstract triangle types. What becomes available is one constructed boundary-triangle instance. What remains refused is any identity claim beyond the selected stations and their realised joins.

Theorem 099 — Twenty Boundary-Triangle Instances From Six Stations

Claim: There are twenty boundary-triangle instances from six stations.

The ninety-ninth theorem counts the boundary-triangle instances made possible by six stations. A triangle instance requires a selection of three distinct boundary stations. The six-station field therefore supplies twenty such selections. The count is a catalogue count, not a geometry theorem about triangle identity. It tells the proof how many station triples may be inspected before any family reduction occurs. What becomes available is a finite instance ledger of twenty boundary triangles. What remains refused is the claim that twenty instances are twenty different kinds, or that counting selections has already classified their relation-patterns.

Theorem 100 — Twenty Boundary-Triangle Instances Are Not Twenty Identity Families

Claim: The twenty boundary-triangle instances are not twenty identity families.

The hundredth theorem separates instance count from identity count. The field has twenty boundary-triangle selections, but different selections may preserve the same gap pattern or relation structure. The proof therefore refuses to equate named station triples with mathematical identities. This is a crucial catalogue discipline: one ledger records how many instances can be chosen, another records which relation families those instances belong to. What becomes available is the distinction between twenty instances and fewer identity families. What remains refused is the naive assumption that every new label creates a new kind of triangle.

Theorem 101 — Boundary Triangle Identity Depends on Gap Pattern

Claim: Boundary triangle identity depends on gap pattern.

The hundred-first theorem gives boundary-triangle identity its first criterion. A triangle chosen from boundary stations is not identified by its letters alone. It is read by the gaps between the selected stations as the sixfold cycle is walked. Those gaps preserve the relation-pattern of the instance, even when the letters or position change. The theorem does not import angle measurement or side-length formulae. It uses the ordered boundary cycle already earned. What becomes available is gap-pattern identity for boundary triangles. What remains refused is identity by name, by drawing appearance, or by unearned school classifications.

Theorem 102 — Three Consecutive Stations Form a 30-120-30 Family Instance

Claim: Three consecutive stations form a 30-120-30 family instance.

The hundred-second theorem inspects the triangle formed by three consecutive boundary stations. Consecutive selection gives two unit boundary steps and a longer closing side across the selected arc. In the turn-field reading, the middle station sits between the other two, producing the familiar narrow-wide-narrow family that will later be called 30-120-30. The proof may use that name as a family label only because the gap pattern has been earned. It does not import trigonometric values or full angle arithmetic. What becomes available is the consecutive-station family instance. The label remains subordinate to the constructional gaps that make it readable.

Theorem 103 — Alternating Stations Form a 60-60-60 Family Instance

Claim: Alternating stations form a 60-60-60 family instance.

The hundred-third theorem looks at alternating boundary stations. Selecting every other station around the sixfold cycle gives three gaps of the same size. The resulting boundary triangle carries equal spacing in the source cycle, and its relation-pattern is the equal-opening family instance later named 60-60-60. The proof does not need mature equilateral theory to recognize the family; it uses the cycle gap record and the recurrence of equal station spacing. What becomes available is the alternating-station identity family. What remains refused is importing all equilateral-triangle consequences before this boundary instance has earned only its pattern.

Theorem 104 — Diameter-Containing Selections Form a 30-60-90 Family Instance

Claim: Diameter-containing selections form a 30-60-90 family instance.

The hundred-fourth theorem turns diameter-containing selections into a distinct family. When a chosen boundary triple includes two opposite stations, the connector between them passes through the center, and the third selected station stands on the same boundary cycle. That pattern is the source of the boundary-triangle family later read as 30-60-90. The proof is not invoking the diameter theorem of classical geometry; it is using opposite-station relation, boundary selection, and the realized triple. What becomes available is a diameter-containing family instance. What remains refused is any right-triangle theorem not yet paid for by the source field.

Theorem 105 — First Circle as Finite Catalogue Frame for Boundary Triangles

Claim: The first circle is a finite catalogue frame for boundary triangles.

The hundred-fifth theorem names the first circle as a finite catalogue frame. Six boundary stations, and only those six selected stations, govern the boundary-triangle selections now under inspection. The frame is finite because the catalogue is built from station triples, not from every possible point on a curve. It is a catalogue because instances can be counted, compared, and sorted by gap pattern. What becomes available is a bounded environment for boundary-triangle reasoning. What remains refused is the infinite circle as an unearned point-set and every classification that ignores the selected-station frame.

Theorem 106 — Catalogue Distinguishes Instance From Identity

Claim: The catalogue distinguishes instance from identity.

The hundred-sixth theorem strengthens the catalogue by separating two jobs. The instance ledger records each selected triple of boundary stations. The identity ledger records the relation pattern that a triple belongs to. A catalogue that cannot make this distinction will either overcount identities or erase real instances. The proof therefore keeps station selection and invariant pattern side by side. What becomes available is a catalogue capable of reading both named occurrence and family. What remains refused is collapsing all positions into one abstraction or, conversely, treating every position as a new mathematical kind.

Theorem 107 — Different Names With Same Identity Family

Claim: Two triangles may have different names but the same identity family.

The hundred-seventh theorem lets different names share one identity family. A triangle may be called by one set of boundary letters, while another triangle uses a different set. If the two selections preserve the same gap pattern, their identity family is the same despite the change of names. The proof does not erase the instances; it refuses to confuse labels with structural difference. What becomes available is name-change tolerance under preserved relation-pattern. This prepares later rotation and reflection readings, but it does not yet import transformation theory. It only says that different station names may carry the same family identity when the source pattern is preserved.

Theorem 108 — Different Positions May Preserve Relation Pattern

Claim: Two triangles may occupy different positions but preserve relation pattern.

The hundred-eighth theorem adds position to the same discipline. Two triangle instances may sit in different places around the boundary and still preserve the same relation pattern. The proof follows the ordered cycle rather than the visual location alone: if the gaps match, the family is preserved even though the instance has moved. This gives the catalogue a way to notice invariance without importing symmetry as a completed theory. What becomes available is position-change with family preservation. What remains refused is the assumption that sameness of appearance or difference of location is enough without the gap pattern being checked.

Theorem 109 — Rotation Preserves Family While Changing Triangle Letters

Claim: A rotation can change triangle letters while preserving family.

The hundred-ninth theorem allows rotation to be read cautiously. A rotation can carry a boundary-triangle instance to different boundary letters while preserving the gap pattern that defines its family. The theorem does not introduce a rotation group or treat transformations as abstract objects. It reads rotation as a relation-preserving movement within the sixfold cycle. What becomes available is the idea that triangle letters may change while family remains stable. What remains refused is any mature symmetry theory beyond this source-bound fact: the carried instance keeps its family because the station-gap structure is preserved.

Theorem 110 — Reflection Reverses Orientation While Preserving Family

Claim: A reflection can reverse orientation while preserving family.

The hundred-tenth theorem gives reflection an equally bounded role. Reflection may reverse the orientation of a triangle instance, so the cyclic order is read the other way. Yet the gap pattern can remain the same, and the family identity can therefore survive the reversal. The proof does not claim all mirror symmetry facts or import congruence doctrine. It records a precise catalogue behaviour: orientation may reverse while the underlying family pattern remains. What becomes available is reflected family preservation. What remains refused is treating orientation reversal as either destructive by default or as a license for full transformation geometry.

Theorem 111 — Triangle Catalogue Is Local, Finite, and Construction-Bound

Claim: The triangle catalogue is local, finite, and construction-bound.

The hundred-eleventh theorem fixes the scale of the triangle catalogue. It is local because it belongs to the first six-station boundary. It is finite because station triples can be counted. It is construction-bound because no triangle enters unless its stations and joins are supplied by the field. This keeps the catalogue from pretending to classify all triangles in mathematics. Its strength lies in the opposite direction: it is small enough to be audited completely. What becomes available is a disciplined local catalogue. What remains refused is any total triangle taxonomy or universal geometry claim smuggled into this first finite frame.

Theorem 112 — Catalogue as Seed Catalogue Rather Than Total Mathematics

Claim: The catalogue is a seed catalogue rather than a total mathematics.

The hundred-twelfth theorem protects the catalogue from overclaiming. The first boundary catalogue is a seed catalogue, not the whole of mathematics. It shows how instances, names, positions, patterns, rotations, and reflections can be distinguished inside a source-bound field. That is powerful because it gives later catalogues a method. But it does not exhaust triangle theory, plane geometry, or transformation theory. What becomes available is a model of catalogue reasoning that may grow. What remains refused is mistaking the first finite catalogue for a final classification of every possible mathematical figure.

Theorem 113 — Triangle Identity Readable After Station Selection

Claim: Triangle identity becomes readable only after station selection exists.

The hundred-thirteenth theorem returns identity to its source condition. A boundary-triangle identity becomes readable only after the three stations have been selected. Before selection, there is no instance to inspect, no gap pattern to record, and no family to assign. The proof therefore places station selection before classification. This prevents the catalogue from floating above the construction as a list of expected types. What becomes available is selection-first identity reading. What remains refused is naming a triangle family before the stations that realize it have been chosen from the sixfold boundary.

Theorem 114 — First Circle Separates Triangle Instances Station Selection

Claim: The first circle separates triangle instances by station selection.

The hundred-fourteenth theorem says how the first circle separates instances. Each boundary triangle is tied to a particular selection of three stations. Different selections therefore produce different instances even when they later fall into the same identity family. The first circle separates by station choice because the stations are ordered and available as distinct constructional roles. What becomes available is instance individuation by selected stations. What remains refused is premature merging of triangles merely because their family pattern will later match. The catalogue must let each instance appear before it decides which invariance it shares.

Theorem 115 — First Circle Unifies Triangle Instances Invariant Family

Claim: The first circle unifies triangle instances by invariant family.

The hundred-fifteenth theorem balances the previous separation with unification. The same first circle that separates triangle instances by station selection can also unify them by invariant family. When different triples preserve the same gap pattern, the catalogue gathers them under one family reading. The proof therefore holds difference and sameness together without confusion. What becomes available is family unification inside the finite boundary catalogue. What remains refused is both extremes: treating all station selections as unrelated, or treating all visually similar triangles as the same without an earned invariant.

Theorem 116 — Triangular Field Begins Unit Relation Repeats Outward

Claim: The triangular field begins when the unit relation repeats outward from lawful stations.

The hundred-sixteenth theorem opens the larger triangular field. The field begins when the unit relation that made the first cells lawful repeats outward from lawful stations. A station beyond the first circle may support the same fixed-opening relation only when construction has carried the relevant conditions there. This is not decoration around the original diagram; it is recurrence of the preserved unit. What becomes available is outward field growth under lawful repetition. What remains refused is any extension that merely copies the picture without preserving stationhood, direction-family structure, and equal-opening evidence.

Theorem 117 — Field Larger Decoration First Circle

Claim: The field is not a larger decoration of the first circle.

The hundred-seventeenth theorem refuses a tempting visual reading. The field is not a larger decoration of the first circle, as though the original figure had simply been surrounded by more drawn triangles. It is a proof field only where operations recur lawfully. Stations, unit edges, and cells must each be earned by the same preservation rules that governed the beginning. What becomes available is a stricter understanding of field extension. The field grows by repeated source conditions, not by ornament. What remains refused is visual expansion without operational permission.

Theorem 118 — Field Recurrence First Unit Relation

Claim: The field is the recurrence of the first unit relation.

The hundred-eighteenth theorem states the field in its simplest form. The field is the recurrence of the first unit relation. The fixed opening that reached from center to boundary and from boundary station to boundary station can recur at other lawful stations. Each recurrence keeps the unit relation alive in a new place. The proof does not require a grid, coordinate plane, or lattice theory to say this. It says only that the unit relation repeats where station authority and preserved opening permit it. What becomes available is a field read as recurrence. What remains refused is treating the field as a pre-existing space into which units are merely drawn.

Theorem 119 — Every Lawful Field Edge Remembers Preserved Opening

Claim: Every lawful field edge remembers the preserved opening.

The hundred-nineteenth theorem gives every lawful field edge a memory. An edge in the field is not simply a drawn segment between two nearby marks. It is lawful only when it remembers the preserved opening that made it. That memory may be direct, through the original reach, or transported through preserved local conditions. The proof therefore ties field edges back to the first unit evidence. What becomes available is unit-memory for lawful edges. What remains refused is any edge whose length or role is assumed by appearance rather than witnessed by the constructional history of the preserved opening.

Theorem 120 — Equilateral Cells Recur Throughout Field

Claim: Equilateral cells recur throughout the field.

The hundred-twentieth theorem lets equal-opening cells recur through the field. Wherever three station-pairs reproduce the defining relations of the first central cell, the equal-opening triangle proof may travel. The cell is not cloned by sight; it recurs because its side witnesses and station structure recur. This keeps the triangular field from becoming a pattern of merely similar marks. What becomes available is recurring native equal-opening cells throughout the lawful field. What remains refused is general tessellation theory or congruence language that outruns the preserved-opening evidence.

Theorem 121 — Sixfold Neighbourhoods Recur Around Lawful Stations

Claim: Sixfold neighbourhoods recur around lawful stations.

The hundred-twenty-first theorem shows that local neighbourhoods can recur. Around a lawful station, the fixed opening may reach unit neighbours in the six directions supplied by the field. When those relations stand, the station has a sixfold neighbourhood analogous to the one first seen around O. The proof does not say every point of the plane has such a neighbourhood. It says lawful stations may receive one when the construction supplies the repeated unit relations. What becomes available is recurring sixfold local structure. What remains refused is universal grid language before stationhood has been earned.

Theorem 122 — Local Centrehood Travel O Another Lawful Station

Claim: Local centrehood can travel from O to another lawful station.

The hundred-twenty-second theorem lets centrehood travel. O was the first center because the initial sweep began there. Another lawful station can take the same local role when it receives the fixed opening and supports the same pattern of unit reaches. This does not erase O or make all points centers automatically. It separates the role of centerhood from the original name O and allows the role to recur under preserved conditions. What becomes available is travelling local centrehood. What remains refused is arbitrary recentering without station authority and fixed-opening recurrence.

Theorem 123 — Local Station Receive Fixed Opening O Did

Claim: A local station can receive the fixed opening as O did.

The hundred-twenty-third theorem focuses on the receiving station. A local station can receive the fixed opening as O did when the construction has made that station lawful and the compass action may be placed there. The station then supports a sweep or reach under the preserved unit relation. The proof does not claim that every location can behave like O; it claims that the role is reusable where the source conditions are preserved. What becomes available is local reception of the fixed opening. What remains refused is treating centerhood as a visual privilege or coordinate convenience rather than an earned constructional role.

Theorem 124 — First Circle Relation Recur Around Another Station

Claim: The first circle relation can recur around another station.

The hundred-twenty-fourth theorem allows the first circle relation to recur around another station. If a lawful station receives the fixed opening as a local center, positions reached by that opening form a boundary relation around it just as the first sweep did around O. This is recurrence, not historical repetition. The original circle remains the source model, while the new local relation stands only where its conditions are re-earned. What becomes available is local circle-relation recurrence. What remains refused is a global circle theory or the assumption that every station automatically carries every consequence of O without proof.

Theorem 125 — Field Repeats Local Conditions Rather Than Copying

Claim: The field repeats local conditions rather than copying the original diagram wholesale.

The hundred-twenty-fifth theorem distinguishes field repetition from diagram copying. The field repeats local conditions: preserved opening, lawful stationhood, unit reach, direction recurrence, and cell formation. It does not copy the original drawing wholesale, and it does not require every visual feature of the first circle to be pasted everywhere. This protects the field from becoming a wallpaper. What becomes available is condition-based repetition. What remains refused is resemblance-based transfer. Later proof may move confidently through the field only by showing which source relations survived the move.

Theorem 126 — Unit Edges Recur Nearestneighbour Relations

Claim: Unit edges recur as nearest-neighbour relations.

The hundred-twenty-sixth theorem identifies unit edges as nearest-neighbour relations. In the triangular field, a lawful station has adjacent stations reached by one preserved opening. The edge between such stations is a unit edge because it spends the fixed-opening evidence. It is nearest-neighbour in the native field sense: one unit step away along the field’s own structure. The proof does not import metric topology or graph theory, though both may later recognize the pattern. What becomes available is unit-neighbour language. What remains refused is any claim that visual proximity alone establishes nearest-neighbour status.

Theorem 127 — Line Families Recur Through Field

Claim: Line families recur through the field.

The hundred-twenty-seventh theorem lets line families recur through the field. Direction families were first exposed in the sixfold boundary; as the field extends, the same preserved bearings can continue through repeated stations. A line family is therefore not imposed by analytic equations. It is carried by station-to-station recurrence along one direction. What becomes available is recurring carrier structure across the triangular field. What remains refused is full parallel-line theory, slope algebra, or coordinate line formulae before the field has supplied the relevant station families.

Theorem 128 — Distance Classes Recur Through Field

Claim: Distance classes recur through the field.

The hundred-twenty-eighth theorem observes that distance classes recur through the field. The unit class recurs as nearest-neighbour edges, and larger span classes can recur when station differences repeat the same field relation. The proof does not yet import a complete metric theory. It says only that span evidence can appear again where the native field repeats the relevant station differences. What becomes available is recurring distance-class language under audit. What remains refused is treating any numerical length as field-available before a lawful station difference has realized it.

Theorem 129 — Triangle Identities Recur Through Field

Claim: Triangle identities recur through the field.

The hundred-twenty-ninth theorem lets triangle identities recur beyond the first circle. Once a triangle identity has been read by station selection and relation pattern, the same identity may appear elsewhere in the field when the relevant station gaps and side relations recur. This is not a loose claim of similarity. It is transportability applied to catalogue identity. What becomes available is recurrence of triangle families throughout the lawful triangular field. What remains refused is transferring a triangle name to a new position without checking that the defining relations have survived.

Theorem 130 — Field Be Read Network Carried Equality

Claim: The field can be read as a network of carried equality.

The hundred-thirtieth theorem reads the field as a network of carried equality. Each lawful unit edge carries the preserved opening; each recurring equal-opening cell carries the same side witness pattern; and transported relations remain valid only through preserved conditions. The field can therefore be seen as a network, but the network is not abstract graph theory yet. It is a web of equality evidence carried from station to station. What becomes available is network reading under source discipline. What remains refused is reducing the field to mere connectivity without the equal-opening history that makes the connections meaningful.

Theorem 131 — Field Be Read Fabric Unit Edges

Claim: The field can be read as a fabric of unit edges.

The hundred-thirty-first theorem offers a second field reading: a fabric of unit edges. The triangular field is woven from nearest-neighbour unit relations, each remembering the preserved opening. Adjacent unit edges form cells, and recurring cells give the fabric its regularity. This is not textile metaphor replacing proof; it is a way to see how local unit evidence interlocks across the field. What becomes available is fabric reading as unit-edge continuity. What remains refused is any claim that the fabric is continuous, infinite, or metric in a mature sense before the construction has earned those extensions.

Theorem 132 — Field Be Read Repeated Stationsource Structure

Claim: The field can be read as repeated station-source structure.

It becomes a system of stations capable of sourcing further construction. What becomes available is repeated station-source structure, a way to understand field growth from within. What remains refused is the idea that sourcehood belongs to every point by default or that coordinates can grant it after the fact.

Claim: The field permits selection beyond the first circle.

Theorem 138 — Six Firstcircle Offsets Six Unitneighbour Directions

Theorem 140 — Six Unit Directions Come Three Opposite Pairs

Theorem 142 — Native Carrier Families Q Constant R Constant

Theorem 143 — Coordinate Pair Records Reachable Unit Steps

Theorem 144 — Coordinate Reading Field Field Itself

Claim: A coordinate is a reading of the field, not the field itself.

Theorem 145 — Coordinate Step Another Role Preserved Unit

Theorem 146 — Coordinate Ledger Protects Lawful Station Identity

Theorem 148 — Translating Stations Native Vector Preserves Relative Differences

Claim: Translating all stations by the same native vector preserves relative differences.

Translation enters only as an audit fact about records. If two lawful stations have native coordinate pairs, their relation is recorded by a native difference. The proof is not importing transformation geometry or treating the plane as a free background. Theorem 149 — Native Coordinates Preserve Triangular Lattice Rather Than Square Grid

Claim: Native coordinates preserve the triangular lattice rather than imposing a square grid.

Theorem 150 — Coordinate Difference Records Station Separation

Claim: Coordinate difference records station separation.

Station separation now receives its simplest ledger form. A first lawful station has a native pair record, and a second lawful station has another. The proof does not convert this into ordinary Euclidean distance yet, nor into a free vector-space claim. It only gives separation a durable native record. Theorem 151 — Unit-Neighbour Offsets Are Native Differences of Squared Norm One

Claim: Unit-neighbour offsets are precisely the native differences of squared norm one.

The unit-neighbour offsets are now separated from larger station differences. Around a station, a unit neighbour is reached by one preserved unit step. Theorem 154 — Coordinate Ledger Turns Constructional Location Into Durable Record

Theorem 156 — Native Squared Norm

Claim: The native squared norm is q^2 + q*r + r^2.

The native squared norm is introduced as a record fitted to the triangular field. A native difference has the form (q,r), and its size is not read through a square-grid formula imposed from outside. The triangular carrier structure requires its own audit expression: q^2 + q*r + r^2. This expression records span in the native directions that the field has earned. The proof therefore gives a metric memory without surrendering to Cartesian axes. What becomes available is the native squared norm, a source-bound size record for lawful coordinate differences in the triangular substrate.

Theorem 157 — Native Norm Records Straight Distance in Triangular Field

Claim: The native norm records straight distance in the triangular field.

With the squared norm active, the field can begin to record straight distance between lawful stations. This is not step-count distance, and it is not a measurement imported before the field exists. It is an audit of already earned station separation. What crystallises is native straight-distance memory: the field may compare spans by their squared norm while keeping construction prior to numerical reading.

Theorem 158 — Value One Identifies Unit-Distance Offsets

Claim: The value one identifies unit-distance offsets.

Theorem 160 — Native Distance Depends on Coordinate Difference, Not Absolute Name

Claim: Native distance depends on coordinate difference, not absolute name.

Native distance is now protected from absolute labels. Two stations may have different coordinate names, but the distance between them is read from their difference, not from either name alone. This matters because it keeps the ledger relational. The proof does not ask where a station is in some external plane; it asks how one lawful station stands from another. What becomes available is distance by native difference, independent of absolute naming.

Theorem 161 — Field Does Not Permit All Possible Lengths as Native Station Distances

The native field now refuses a tempting excess. Once the norm can record station distances, it might seem that any numerical length may enter. The field says no. Numbers not produced by lawful differences remain outside the station-distance ledger, however familiar they may be elsewhere. What crystallises is a refusal of imported length freedom. Theorem 162 — Native Norm Produces Distance Classes

Claim: The native norm produces distance classes.

The native norm does more than measure one segment at a time. It gathers lawful differences that share the same squared value into distance classes. Theorem 166 — First Native Squared Distance Values

Theorem 168 — Segment Between Lawful Stations Classified by Native Squared Norm

Theorem 169 — Straight Distance and Step Distance Distinct Readings

Claim: Straight distance and step distance are distinct readings.

The proof now separates two ways of reading separation. Step distance counts movement through unit edges in the field. Straight distance reads the native norm of the coordinate difference between two stations. At the first unit edge these readings may coincide, but they are not the same operation. This distinction prevents path-counting from being confused with direct span. The theorem therefore keeps the ledger honest: a route through cells and a straight station-to-station distance answer different questions. What stands is a two-reading discipline, with step paths and native straight distance both available but not interchangeable.

Theorem 170 — First Circle Straight Unit Distance and Field-Step Coincide

Claim: At the first circle, straight unit distance and one field-step coincide.

At the first circle, the two readings meet in the simplest case. A boundary neighbour is reached by one preserved unit step from the center, and its native difference has squared norm one. The proof does not generalize this coincidence to every separation. It records the controlled place where the readings agree. Theorem 172 — Compass Circle Differs from Step-Shell

Claim: A compass circle differs from a step-shell.

A compass circle and a step-shell now separate. A compass circle is generated by a fixed opening around a center. Theorem 173 — Step-Shell Differs from Straight-Distance Class

Claim: A step-shell differs from a straight-distance class.

The step-shell must also be separated from the straight-distance class. A step-shell gathers stations by route length through unit edges. The proof does not let path accessibility become distance equality. It keeps the route ledger apart from the norm ledger. Theorem 174 — Drawing Becomes Number Filter Once Native Norm Is Active

Claim: The drawing becomes a number filter once the native norm is active.

Once the native norm is active, the drawing becomes more than a picture. It becomes a filter through which numerical claims must pass. Theorem 175 — Native Norm Lets Field Refuse Imported Lengths

Theorem 176 — Distance Claim Must Be Witnessed by Native Difference

Claim: A distance claim must be witnessed by a native difference.

Every distance claim now needs an endpoint receipt. Without those conditions, the claim has no source inside the field. The proof does not accept a distance because it sounds mathematically reasonable or appears on a diagram. It asks for the native difference that witnesses it. What becomes available is a hard standard for later work: a distance claim lands only when a lawful difference carries it.

Theorem 177 — Squared Distance Is Safer First Record Than Distance

Claim: Squared distance is the first safer distance record than distance.

The field records squared distance before it trusts distance names. The proof therefore keeps the squared record as the safer first landing. This is not avoidance; it is discipline. The squared value is the native audit. The root name can come later when needed. What stands is squared distance as the first secure distance record in the ledger.

Theorem 178 — Square-Root Notation Arrives After Squared Native Values

Claim: Square-root notation arrives only after squared native values are recorded.

Square-root notation is allowed only after the field has recorded a squared native value. Theorem 179 — Unit Circle Vocabulary Must Wait

Claim: Unit-circle vocabulary must wait.

The phrase unit circle must wait because it carries too much mature language. The field has a fixed opening, a first boundary, six unit-neighbour offsets, and a native norm value one. Those permissions are real, but they are not yet the full analytic object usually called the unit circle. The proof keeps the vocabulary from outrunning its source. It may speak of first boundary, unit offsets, or native norm class one where those have been earned. What is refused is the imported unit-circle package. Theorem 180 — Native Circle Means Norm Class Around Station

Claim: A native circle means a norm class around a station.

When circle language returns in native form, it means a norm class around a station. A center station is chosen, lawful station differences from that center are inspected, and those with the same squared norm belong to the same native circle class. This is not automatically the same as a compass sweep and not an analytic equation imposed on the plane. It is a ledger definition grounded in native differences. What becomes available is native circle language: a circle as a station-centered distance class inside the triangular field, controlled by the norm rather than by imported coordinate geometry.

Theorem 181 — Compass Circle and Native Circle Agree Only at Controlled Places

Claim: Compass circle and native circle agree only at controlled places.

The compass circle and the native circle may agree, but only where the proof has earned the agreement. A compass circle comes from a fixed sweep. A native circle comes from a norm class around a station. At the first unit boundary, the six unit offsets can link these readings in a controlled way. Beyond such places, the two notions must not be collapsed. The theorem preserves both source acts by keeping their agreement conditional. What stands is a careful bridge: compass sweep and norm class may coincide where witnessed, but neither is allowed to replace the other globally.

Theorem 182 — Coordinate Ledger Is Not Authorization for Cartesian Imports

Claim: The coordinate ledger is not authorization for Cartesian imports.

The coordinate ledger now needs a strong guardrail. Because the field has coordinate records, it may be tempting to import Cartesian axes, equations, slopes, and the whole analytic plane. The proof refuses that move. Native coordinates record a triangular substrate with three carrier families and six unit-neighbour directions. They authorize audit, not replacement. What becomes available is coordinate use under source discipline. What remains refused is Cartesian import by association: a coordinate pair in this book does not open the door to an external coordinate geometry unless the field itself has earned the reading.

Theorem 183 — Cartesian Projection Is Later Reading, Not Source

Claim: Cartesian projection is a later reading, not a source.

Cartesian projection can be useful later, but it cannot be the source here. The triangular field already has lawful stations, native directions, carrier families, and a squared norm before any projection into a rectangular frame is considered. A Cartesian representation may audit, visualize, or compare after the fact. It may not generate the stations or authorize the relations. The theorem therefore gives projection a lower rank than construction. What stands is a disciplined future permission: Cartesian projection may be a later reading of the native field, but it is not the field's origin.

Theorem 184 — Orthogonal Axes Are Not Native Axes

Claim: Orthogonal axes are not native axes.

The native axes are not orthogonal axes. They arise from the three opposite pairs of native unit directions and the three carrier families of the triangular field. Orthogonal axes belong to a later Cartesian reading and carry a different structure. The proof refuses to let two rectangular axes overwrite the three-family organization already earned. This protects the field's own geometry: its carriers come from constructional recurrence, not from a square grid. What becomes available is a clean separation between native axes and later orthogonal axes, allowing audit without confusing one coordinate order for another.

Theorem 185 — Native Pair Has Three Carrier Families Not Two

Claim: A native pair has three carrier families, not two.

A native coordinate pair may have two entries, but the field it records has three carrier families. This is a subtle point and the proof makes it explicit. The pair (q,r) records station position, while the carrier conditions q constant, r constant, and q+r constant express the three directions of the triangular substrate. The record is therefore not a disguised square-grid pair. It belongs to a three-family field. What crystallises is native-pair discipline: two numbers may record a station, but the station lives inside three recurring carrier families, not two Cartesian axes.

Theorem 186 — Cartesian Plane Can Audit But Cannot Replace

Claim: The Cartesian plane can audit but cannot replace the native field.

The Cartesian plane is allowed only as an auditor. It may later help display the native field, check relations, or compare measurements, but it cannot replace the construction that made the field lawful. The native stations, carriers, and norm arise from the triangular substrate. If a Cartesian reading is used, it must answer to those prior facts. The proof therefore gives analytic convenience a bounded office. What becomes available is external audit under native authority. What remains refused is substitution: the Cartesian plane may look clean, but it is not the source field of this proof.

Theorem 187 — Native Coordinate Phase Closes

Claim: The native coordinate phase closes when lawful station recording, native directions, carrier families, coordinate difference, and native norm have been established without replacing the construction.

The native coordinate phase closes by gathering its own discipline. Lawful stations can now be recorded. Unit directions and carrier families have been named. Coordinate differences can record separation, and the native squared norm can classify straight distance. At the same time, every paragraph has preserved the order of authority: construction first, coordinate audit after. This closure does not finish the book; it completes a phase of memory. What becomes available is a stable native coordinate ledger, strong enough for later algebraic and geometric use, yet still unable to replace the source construction that made it meaningful.

Theorem 188 — Algebra Begins When Relation Can Be Recorded Without Redrawing

Claim: Algebra begins when a relation can be recorded without redrawing the diagram.

Algebra begins quietly. A relation already earned in the field can now be recorded so that the diagram need not be redrawn every time the relation is used. This does not detach the relation from its source. It gives the relation a portable memory form. The proof has already learned how coordinates remember stations; now it lets symbols remember relations. What becomes available is the first algebraic act: preserving a field relation in a record that can travel. Algebra enters not as manipulation for its own sake, but as memory of constructional truth.

Theorem 189 — Preserved Opening Becomes Symbolic Unit

Claim: The preserved opening becomes a symbolic unit.

The preserved compass opening can now be held symbolically as a unit. At the beginning it was a physical relation kept by the compass. Through boundary, station, step, cell, coordinate, and norm work, that preserved relation has become the root of the unit edge class. Algebra may now record it as a unit without forgetting where it came from. The proof does not invent one as an abstract number. It lets the first fixed opening receive symbolic memory. What stands is the symbolic unit as a record of preserved constructional sameness.

Theorem 190 — Equality Sign Records Preserved Sameness Claim

Claim: The equality sign records a preserved sameness claim.

The equality sign enters under source pressure. It is not a decorative mark between expressions. It records a claim that some sameness has been preserved: the same opening, the same unit relation, the same norm value, or the same relation carried under lawful conditions. The proof therefore gives equality a duty. It must remember an earned preservation, not announce a wish. What becomes available is equality as a symbolic record of sameness. Later algebraic lines may use the sign only when the relation on both sides remains the same relation under the field's discipline.

Theorem 191 — Variable Marks Unknown Station or Length Role

Claim: A variable marks an unknown station role or length role.

A variable now marks a role that has not yet been recovered. It may stand for an unknown station role, a length role, or another relation inside the source-bound field. The proof does not let the variable become an empty license to manipulate. It is a placeholder for something that must fit the already preserved conditions. The unknown is therefore not wild; it is constrained by the relation in which it appears. What becomes available is variable language as disciplined incompletion: a role may be unnamed for the moment, but it must be recoverable within the lawful field.

Theorem 192 — Equation Records Relation To Be Preserved

Claim: An equation records a relation to be preserved.

An equation is now read as a preservation demand. It records a relation that must continue to stand while the proof works on it. The equation may include known units, native differences, norm values, or variables marking unknown roles, but its authority comes from the relation it preserves. The proof does not treat the equation as a symbol game detached from construction. It is a compact memory of a field condition. What becomes available is equation language: a relation can be held, inspected, and later transformed, provided every step keeps the same underlying relation alive.

Theorem 193 — Solving Means Recovering Compatible Role

Claim: Solving means recovering a compatible role.

Solving now receives its source-bound meaning. To solve is not to force a symbol into a preferred shape; it is to recover the station role, length role, or relation role compatible with the recorded equation. The unknown must fit the preservation conditions already written into the relation. Each algebraic move is therefore judged by whether it keeps compatibility intact. What crystallises is solving as role recovery. The proof gains the ability to find what may stand in an unknown place, while refusing any manipulation that produces an answer no longer answerable to the original relation.

Theorem 194 — Transformation Legal When Relation Preserved

Claim: A transformation is legal when the relation is preserved.

Algebraic transformation enters by a simple test: does the relation remain preserved? A line may be rewritten, rearranged, or carried into another form only when the same underlying equality or condition still stands. The proof does not grant permission because the operation is familiar from school algebra. It asks whether the source relation survives the move. What becomes available is legal transformation under invariance. Algebra may now change expression-form, but it may not change truth-content. The field has gained symbolic motion, and that motion is lawful only while preservation remains visible.

Theorem 195 — Illegal Transformation Changes Source Relation

Claim: An illegal transformation changes the source relation.

The companion refusal is just as important. A transformation is illegal when it changes the source relation it was supposed to preserve. The expression may look neater, and the step may resemble familiar algebra, but if the underlying equality, constraint, or field condition has shifted, the proof rejects it. This gives algebra a source-bound conscience. It cannot trade truth for convenience. What stands is a clear standard of failure: a symbolic move that alters the relation has left the proof. Later manipulation must therefore carry a receipt of preservation, not merely a recognizable algebraic shape.

Theorem 196 — Algebraic Manipulation Must Carry Invariant

Claim: Algebraic manipulation must carry an invariant.

Every algebraic manipulation now has to carry an invariant. Something must remain the same through the change: an equality, a norm value, a station relation, a unit role, or another recorded condition. Without that carried sameness, manipulation becomes drift. The proof therefore binds algebra to the same discipline that governed construction. A step may transform the record, but it must preserve what made the record true. What becomes available is invariant-carrying manipulation: symbolic work can move quickly, but only because the invariant keeps the source relation standing through each change.

Theorem 197 — Formula Is Portable Proof Memory

Claim: A formula is portable proof memory.

A formula now becomes portable proof memory. It gathers a relation that has been earned, records the conditions under which it holds, and allows that relation to be reused without rebuilding the entire argument from the beginning. This portability is not freedom from source. A formula remains valid only where its conditions are preserved. The proof therefore treats formulae as disciplined carriers, not detached authorities. What becomes available is compact reuse: a field relation may travel in symbolic form, provided the formula remembers the proof that made it true and the conditions under which it may be spent.

Theorem 198 — Formula Is Not Detached Authority

Claim: A formula is not detached authority.

The formula is immediately put under restraint. It may be useful, compact, and portable, but it is not an authority detached from its proof. A formula cannot be applied merely because its shape resembles a situation. The source conditions that produced it must still be present. The proof refuses the common shortcut of letting a remembered expression replace judgment. What stands is formula discipline: symbolic memory may help the proof move, but it may not govern the field from outside. A formula remains answerable to the constructional and relational permissions it records.

Theorem 199 — Algebraic Record Can Travel Where Conditions Preserved

Claim: An algebraic record can travel where its conditions are preserved.

An algebraic record may travel, but only under preserved conditions. If a relation was recorded in one part of the field, another part may use it when the station roles, unit relations, norm classes, or other defining conditions match. The proof is applying transportability to symbolic memory. The record moves because the source relation moves with it, not because the symbols are attractive or familiar. What becomes available is lawful algebraic transport. Later algebra can work across the field, but each journey must carry the conditions that made the original record true.

Theorem 200 — Algebraic Record Cannot Travel By Shape Resemblance

Claim: An algebraic record cannot travel by shape resemblance.

The field also refuses algebraic resemblance as proof. A symbolic record cannot be applied to a new situation merely because the expression looks similar. Shape may suggest a possible use, but it does not authorize one. The proof asks for preserved conditions: matching roles, relations, invariants, and source permissions. Without them, the algebraic record must stay where it was earned. What crystallises is non-arbitrary symbolic transport. Algebra gains reach, but not by visual pattern alone. It may travel only when the field can show that the same relation is still standing.

Theorem 201 — Opposite Directions Belong to Axis Family

Claim: Opposite directions belong to the same axis family.

Opposite directions are now gathered under one axis family. The six unit directions have already been paired into three opposite pairs, and each pair records two orientations along one carrier family. The proof does not turn this into Cartesian axis theory. It simply lets a direction and its opposite belong to the same unoriented native family. What becomes available is axis-family membership for opposite directions. A later line or carrier reading may choose an orientation when needed, but the family itself holds both directions as one native axis relation.

Theorem 202 — Native Line Families Generated by Lawful Station Alignment

Claim: Native line families are generated by lawful station alignment.

Native line families now arise from aligned lawful stations. A direction family supplies a repeated bearing, and stations generated by the field may align along that bearing. When station after station belongs to the same carrier relation, a native line family is generated. The proof does not import the full theory of lines or the Cartesian equation of a line. It watches lawful alignment persist through the substrate. What stands is native line-family generation: a line family is a constructional carrier of stations, not an abstract object laid over the field from outside.

Theorem 203 — Carrier Membership Can Be Tested by Coordinate Condition

Claim: Carrier membership can be tested by coordinate condition.

The coordinate ledger can now test whether a station belongs to a carrier. Because the native field has three carrier families, membership can be checked by conditions such as q constant, r constant, or q+r constant. The proof is careful again: the condition does not generate the carrier; it audits a carrier already earned by direction-family recurrence. What becomes available is a precise membership test. A station can be checked against a native line family by coordinate condition, while the carrier itself remains grounded in lawful station alignment and field recurrence.

Theorem 204 — Field Edge Lies Along Native Carrier Direction

Claim: A field edge lies along one native carrier direction.

A field edge now receives its directional reading. A unit edge joins neighbouring lawful stations, and its difference belongs to one of the native unit directions. That direction is part of a carrier family. The proof therefore reads each field edge as lying along a native carrier direction without importing angle measure, slope, or Euclidean line theory. What becomes available is edge-carrier alignment: every lawful unit edge carries one of the substrate's native direction relations. Later paths and line-family arguments may spend this fact, but only where the edge has been constructionally supplied.

Theorem 205 — Chain Unit Edges Trace Native Line Family

Claim: A chain of unit edges can trace a native line family.

A chain of unit edges can now trace a native line family when each edge continues along the same carrier direction. The proof builds the chain locally: one unit edge, then the next, then the next, always preserving the carrier relation. It does not declare an infinite line by fiat, and it does not import analytic straightness. It lets repeated unit-edge alignment produce a trace through lawful stations. What crystallises is native line tracing by edge chain. The field can now follow a carrier family step by step, with every station and edge paid for by the substrate.

Theorem 206 — Diameters First Circle Lie Along Three Native

Claim: Diameters of the first circle lie along the three native axes.

The first circle now yields its through-center readings. Opposite boundary stations have already been earned, and a connector between each opposite pair passes through O. Those connectors do not arrive as imported diameters from circle doctrine; they arise from the six-step cycle and its opposite station relations. Because the sixfold field contains three opposite pairs, the first circle displays three such through-center carriers. Each lies along one native axis family. What becomes available is diameter-as-native-axis evidence: the field can read its first circle through three through-center axes, while general diameter theory and coordinate-axis habit remain outside the source boundary.

Theorem 207 — Each Opposite Station Pair Displays Native Axis

Claim: Each opposite station pair displays one native axis through O.

Each opposite station pair now receives an axis reading. A selected boundary station and its third-step opposite are related by the sixfold cycle, and their connector passes through O. That through-center relation gives one native axis, not because a Cartesian frame has been placed over the figure, but because the substrate itself supplies two opposed directions along one carrier. The proof therefore lets each opposite pair display one native axis through the original center. What stands is axis evidence grounded in station opposition, center passage, and field recurrence. Every external axis system remains refused until the substrate has earned it.

Theorem 208 — Parallel Carrier Lines Recur Through Field

Claim: Parallel carrier lines recur through the field.

The field can now notice recurrence beyond a single carrier. A native direction family has already been extended, and lawful station alignment can trace carriers through repeated stations. When the same carrier direction appears again at another location, the field has parallel-like recurrence. The proof does not spend the mature theorem package of parallel lines. It only records repeated carrier lines of the same native family, each generated by lawful station alignment. What becomes available is parallel carrier recurrence inside the triangular substrate. The word parallel remains disciplined: it names preserved direction-family recurrence, not an imported Euclidean authority.

Theorem 209 — Field Contains Alignment Before External Line Theory

Claim: The field contains alignment before external line theory is invoked.

Alignment now stands before line theory. The substrate already contains stations, unit edges, carrier families, and repeated direction. When stations fall along one carrier relation, the proof may read alignment inside the field itself. It does not need an external definition of a line to make that relation visible. This theorem protects the order of discovery: line language may later compress the relation, but the aligned station sequence comes first. What crystallises is native alignment prior to external line theory. Later geometry may speak more quickly, but its speed must remember the station relations that made alignment lawful.

Theorem 210 — Native Line Station Relation Inside Substrate

Claim: A native line is a station relation inside the substrate.

A native line is now treated as a relation among lawful stations. It is not an object dropped into an empty plane. A carrier direction gathers stations that stand along the same repeated bearing, and the field may read that gathered relation as a native line. The proof therefore keeps linehood attached to construction: station generation, direction-family recurrence, and carrier membership all remain active. What becomes available is line-as-substrate-relation. The field may use native line language where the station relation is present, while abstract line theory, equations, and imported straightness remain later conveniences rather than sources.

Theorem 211 — Carrier Families Organize Station Arrays

Claim: Carrier families organize station arrays.

Carrier families now organize more than isolated paths. As stations recur through the triangular field, each family gathers a repeated array of aligned stations. One carrier holds one line of stations; the family holds the recurrence of such carriers across the field. The proof is not creating a numerical grid from outside. It is reading order that the direction families already produce. What stands is station-array organization by native carriers. Later coordinate reading, decomposition, and comparison may use these arrays, but the arrays remain constructional: they arise from preserved direction and lawful station meeting, not from imposed background axes.

Theorem 212 — Carrier Families Support Decomposition Cuts

Claim: Carrier families support decomposition cuts.

Carrier families can now guide lawful cuts. A body built from cells may be read through the directions that already organize the substrate. When a cut follows a carrier family, it travels along a line of lawful station relations rather than across the figure by visual convenience. The proof does not yet open all decomposition theory. It gives one source-bound permission: carrier direction may support a cut where the relevant stations and edges have been supplied. What becomes available is decomposition by native carrier. A later body can be divided along the field's own structure, not by an arbitrary slash drawn over it.

Theorem 213 — Carrier Families Support Coordinate Reading

Claim: Carrier families support coordinate reading.

Coordinate reading gains a native scaffold from the carrier families. The field already has three recurring direction families, and station arrays are organized by them. A coordinate record can therefore remember station position by reference to the carriers that construction supplied. The ledger is not inventing axes; it is reading families that already stand. This makes coordinate use safer, because each coordinate condition answers to a native carrier relation. What becomes available is carrier-supported coordinate reading, a way for the ledger to track the substrate without turning into the source of the substrate.

Theorem 214 — Carrier Families Support Symmetry Inspection

Claim: Carrier families support symmetry inspection.

Symmetry can now be inspected, but not assumed. Carrier families let the proof compare opposite directions, repeated station arrays, and through-center relations. If a proposed symmetry preserves the relevant carriers and station roles, it can be examined inside the field. If it does not, resemblance is not enough. The theorem therefore gives symmetry a native inspection method without importing a finished symmetry theory. What becomes available is carrier-supported symmetry audit: the field may ask what relations remain standing under a proposed change, while every unearned appeal to visual balance remains refused.

Theorem 215 — Carrier Families Turn Repeated Unit Relations Visible

Claim: Carrier families turn repeated unit relations into visible order.

Repeated unit relations no longer appear as scattered steps. The carrier families gather them into visible order. Along a native carrier, one unit edge may follow another; across the field, the same unit relation may recur in aligned arrays. The proof reads that order from preserved station and edge relations, not from a grid imposed after the fact. What becomes available is ordered unit recurrence: the field can see where unit steps repeat and how they belong to carrier families. Later counting, cutting, and coordinate audit can spend this order, but only where the unit relation itself has been earned.

Theorem 216 — Sameness Name Its Invariant

Claim: Sameness must name its invariant.

The proof now turns toward the grammar of sameness. To say that something is the same is not enough; the unchanged feature must be named. Same opening, same station role, same unit distance, same carrier direction, same area count, and same relation pattern are different kinds of sameness. The theorem therefore refuses vague identity. A claim of sameness must say what survives the comparison or change. What becomes available is invariant-naming discipline. Later transformation, decomposition, and algebraic memory can move only when they carry a named invariant rather than leaning on a loose feeling that two things match.

Theorem 217 — Length Differs Area

Claim: Same length differs from same area.

The first distinction separates length from area. A segment may preserve a span while a cell count changes; two bodies may carry equal area while their boundary lengths differ. The proof does not need mature area theory to make the refusal. It only needs to keep the kind of invariant named. Length records one-dimensional span; area will count lawful cells or accounted pieces. These are not interchangeable. What crystallises is type discipline for sameness: a same-length claim does not prove a same-area claim, and a same-area claim does not prove a same-length claim unless further source relations are supplied.

Theorem 218 — Area Differs Shape

Claim: Same area differs from same shape.

The next distinction separates area from shape. A body may keep the same counted parts while those parts are rearranged into a different outline. If the accounted cells remain preserved, area may be carried; but the resulting shape need not be the same. The proof thereby prevents a familiar shortcut: equal amount of field is not equal form. What becomes available is a clean difference between area invariant and shape invariant. Later recomposition may preserve area while changing outline, but it must not pretend that a preserved count automatically preserves the body's shape.

Theorem 219 — Shape Differs Position

Claim: Same shape differs from same position.

Shape and position now separate. A body may preserve its internal relation pattern while standing somewhere else in the field. The shape can remain readable through its sides, cells, and carried relations, while the station records that locate it have changed. The proof does not import transformation geometry; it names the difference between internal form and field location. What stands is another invariant boundary: same shape is not the same as same position. Later translation and placement arguments can use this distinction, provided they say which relation is preserved and which relation has changed.

Theorem 220 — Identity Family Differs Named Instance

Claim: Same identity family differs from same named instance.
